href="/wiki/Milne_model" title="Milne model">Milne</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" href="/wiki/Van_Stockum_dust" title="Van Stockum dust">van Stockum dust</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Weyl%E2%88%92Lewis%E2%88%92Papapetrou_coordinates" title="Weyl&#8722;Lewis&#8722;Papapetrou relativity)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Vacuum_solution" title="Vacuum solution">Vacuum solution</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:#ddf; text-align:center;">Scientists</div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">Lorentz</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">Hilbert</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincar&#233;">Poincar&#233;</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Karl_Schwarzschild" title="Karl Schwarzschild">Schwarzschild</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Willem_de_Sitter" title="Willem de Sitter">de Sitter</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hans_Reissner" title="Hans Reissner">Reissner</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gunnar_Nordstr%C3%B6m" title="Gunnar Nordstr&#246;m">Nordstr&#246;m</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hermann_Weyl" title="Hermann Weyl">Weyl</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Eddington</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Friedmann" title="Alexander Friedmann">Friedman</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Edward_Arthur_Milne" title="Edward Arthur Milne">Milne</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Zwicky</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lema&#238;tre">Lema&#238;tre</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kurt_G%C3%B6del" title="Kurt G&#246;del">G&#246;del</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/John_Archibald_Wheeler" title="John Archibald Wheeler">Wheeler</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Howard_P._Robertson" title="Howard P. Robertson">Robertson</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/James_M._Bardeen" title="James M. Bardeen">Bardeen</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Geoffrey_Walker" title="Arthur Geoffrey Walker">Walker</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Roy_Kerr" title="Roy Kerr">Kerr</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Subrahmanyan_Chandrasekhar" title="Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar">Chandrasekhar</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Ehlers" title="J&#252;rgen Ehlers">Ehlers</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Roger_Penrose" title="Roger Penrose">Penrose</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Hawking</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Amal_Kumar_Raychaudhuri" title="Amal Kumar Raychaudhuri">Raychaudhuri</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Hooton_Taylor_Jr." title="Joseph Hooton Taylor Jr.">Taylor</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Russell_Alan_Hulse" title="Russell Alan Hulse">Hulse</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Willem_Jacob_van_Stockum" title="Willem Jacob van Stockum">van Stockum</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Abraham_H._Taub" title="Abraham H. Taub">Taub</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ezra_T._Newman" title="Ezra T. Newman">Newman</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Shing-Tung_Yau" title="Shing-Tung Yau">Yau</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kip_Thorne" title="Kip Thorne">Thorne</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_contributors_to_general_relativity" title="List of contributors to general relativity"><i>others</i></a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:General_relativity_sidebar" title="Template:General relativity sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:General_relativity_sidebar" title="Template talk:General relativity sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:General_relativity_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>A <b>black hole</b> is a region of <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a> exhibiting such strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitational</a> effects that nothing&#8212;not even <a href="/wiki/Particle" title="Particle">particles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>&#8212;can escape from inside it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> predicts that a sufficiently compact <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> can deform <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a> to form a black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wald_1997_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wald_1997-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150608_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150608-3">[3]</a></sup> The boundary of the region from which no escape is possible is called the <a href="/wiki/Event_horizon" title="Event horizon">event horizon</a>. Although the event horizon has an enormous effect on the fate and circumstances of an object crossing it, no locally detectable features appear to be observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> In many ways a black hole acts like an ideal <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black body</a>, as it reflects no light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Moreover, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory_in_curved_spacetime" title="Quantum field theory in curved spacetime">quantum field theory in curved spacetime</a> predicts that event horizons emit <a href="/wiki/Hawking_radiation" title="Hawking radiation">Hawking radiation</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">the same spectrum</a> as a black body of a temperature inversely proportional to its mass. This temperature is on the order of billionths of a kelvin for <a href="/wiki/Stellar_black_hole" title="Stellar black hole">black holes of stellar mass</a>, making it essentially impossible to observe.\n</p><p>Objects whose <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">gravitational fields</a> are too strong for light to escape were first considered in the 18th century by <a href="/wiki/John_Michell" title="John Michell">John Michell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace" title="Pierre-Simon Laplace">Pierre-Simon Laplace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-origin_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-origin-7">[7]</a></sup> The first modern solution of general relativity that would characterize a black hole was found by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Schwarzschild" title="Karl Schwarzschild">Karl Schwarzschild</a> in 1916, although its interpretation as a region of space from which nothing can escape was first published by <a href="/wiki/David_Finkelstein" title="David Finkelstein">David Finkelstein</a> in 1958. Black holes were long considered a mathematical curiosity; it was during the 1960s that theoretical work showed they were a generic prediction of general relativity. The discovery of <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a> in the late 1960s sparked interest in <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitationally collapsed</a> compact objects as a possible astrophysical reality.\n</p><p>Black holes of stellar mass are expected to form when very massive stars collapse at the end of their life cycle. After a black hole has formed, it can continue to grow by absorbing mass from its surroundings. By absorbing other stars and merging with other black holes, <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black holes</a> of millions of <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub></a>) may form. There is general consensus that supermassive black holes exist in the centers of most <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a>.\n</p><p>Despite its invisible interior, the presence of a black hole can be inferred through its interaction with other <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and with <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> such as visible light. Matter that falls onto a black hole can form an external <a href="/wiki/Accretion_disk" title="Accretion disk">accretion disk</a> heated by friction, forming some of the <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">brightest objects in the universe</a>. If there are other stars orbiting a black hole, their orbits can be used to determine the black hole\'s mass and location. Such observations can be used to exclude possible alternatives such as neutron stars. In this way, astronomers have identified numerous stellar black hole candidates in <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary systems</a>, and established that the radio source known as <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_A*" title="Sagittarius A*">Sagittarius A*</a>, at the core of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy, contains a supermassive black hole of about 4.3 million <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a>.\n</p><p>On 11 February 2016, the <a href="/wiki/LIGO" title="LIGO">LIGO</a> collaboration <a href="/wiki/First_observation_of_gravitational_waves" title="First observation of gravitational waves">announced the first direct detection</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitational waves</a>, which also represented the first observation of a black hole merger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRL-20160211_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-PRL-20160211-8">[8]</a></sup> As of  December&#160;2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Black_hole&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, eleven <a href="/wiki/List_of_gravitational_wave_observations" title="List of gravitational wave observations">gravitational wave events</a> have been observed that originated from ten merging black holes (along with one binary <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star_merger" title="Neutron star merger">neutron star merger</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2018Dec_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-2018Dec-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ligo_list_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-ligo_list-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BlackHole_Lensing.gif"><img width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BlackHole_Lensing.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Simulation of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a href="#Popular_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Popular reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#University_textbooks_and_monographs"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">University textbooks and monographs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Review_papers"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Review papers</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BH_LMC.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/BH_LMC.png/220px-BH_LMC.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/BH_LMC.png/330px-BH_LMC.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/BH_LMC.png/440px-BH_LMC.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BH_LMC.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Simulated view of a black hole in front of the <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a>. Note the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">gravitational lensing</a> effect, which produces two enlarged but highly distorted views of the Cloud. Across the top, the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> disk appears distorted into an arc.</div></div></div>\n<p>The idea of a body so massive that even light could not escape was briefly proposed by astronomical pioneer and English clergyman <a href="/wiki/John_Michell" title="John Michell">John Michell</a> in a letter published in November 1784. Michell\'s simplistic calculations assumed that such a body might have the same density as the Sun, and concluded that such a body would form when a star\'s diameter exceeds the Sun\'s by a factor of 500, and the surface <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> exceeds the usual speed of light. Michell correctly noted that such supermassive but non-radiating bodies might be detectable through their gravitational effects on nearby visible bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-origin_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-origin-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thorne_123_124_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-thorne_123_124-13">[13]</a></sup> Scholars of the time were initially excited by the proposal that giant but invisible stars might be hiding in plain view, but enthusiasm dampened when the wavelike nature of light became apparent in the early nineteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> If light were a wave rather than a "<a href="/wiki/Particle" title="Particle">corpuscle</a>", it became unclear what, if any, influence gravity would have on escaping light waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-origin_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-origin-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thorne_123_124_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-thorne_123_124-13">[13]</a></sup> Modern relativity discredits Michell\'s notion of a light ray shooting directly from the surface of a supermassive star, being slowed down by the star\'s gravity, stopping, and then free-falling back to the star\'s surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_relativity">General relativity</span></h3>\n<p>In 1915, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> developed his theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, having earlier shown that gravity does influence light\'s motion. Only a few months later, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Schwarzschild" title="Karl Schwarzschild">Karl Schwarzschild</a> found a <a href="/wiki/Schwarzschild_metric" title="Schwarzschild metric">solution</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Einstein_field_equations" title="Einstein field equations">Einstein field equations</a>, which describes the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">gravitational field</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Point_mass" title="Point mass">point mass</a> and a spherical mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwarzschild1916_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwarzschild1916-16">[16]</a></sup> A few months after Schwarzschild, Johannes Droste, a student of <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">Hendrik Lorentz</a>, independently gave the same solution for the point mass and wrote more extensively about its properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> This solution had a peculiar behaviour at what is now called the <a href="/wiki/Schwarzschild_radius" title="Schwarzschild radius">Schwarzschild radius</a>, where it became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematical_singularity" title="Mathematical singularity">singular</a>, meaning that some of the terms in the Einstein equations became infinite. The nature of this surface was not quite understood at the time. In 1924, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Arthur Eddington</a> showed that the singularity disappeared after a change of coordinates (see <a href="/wiki/Eddington%E2%80%93Finkelstein_coordinates" title="Eddington&#8211;Finkelstein coordinates">Eddington&#8211;Finkelstein coordinates</a>), although it took until 1933 for <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lema&#238;tre">Georges Lema&#238;tre</a> to realize that this meant the singularity at the Schwarzschild radius was a non-physical <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_singularity" title="Coordinate singularity">coordinate singularity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HooftHist_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-HooftHist-19">[19]</a></sup> Arthur Eddington did however comment on the possibility of a star with mass compressed to the Schwarzschild radius in a 1926 book, noting that Einstein\'s theory allows us to rule out overly large densities for visible stars like Betelgeuse because "a star of 250 million km radius could not possibly have so high a density as the sun. Firstly, the force of gravitation would be so great that light would be unable to escape from it, the rays falling back to the star like a stone to the earth. Secondly, the red shift of the spectral lines would be so great that the spectrum would be shifted out of existence. Thirdly, the mass would produce so much curvature of the space-time metric that space would close up around the star, leaving us outside (i.e., nowhere)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eddington1926_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-eddington1926-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1931, <a href="/wiki/Subrahmanyan_Chandrasekhar" title="Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar">Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar</a> calculated, using special relativity, that a non-rotating body of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron-degenerate_matter" title="Electron-degenerate matter">electron-degenerate matter</a> above a certain limiting mass (now called the <a href="/wiki/Chandrasekhar_limit" title="Chandrasekhar limit">Chandrasekhar limit</a> at 1.4&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>) has no stable solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venkataraman92_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-venkataraman92-22">[22]</a></sup> His arguments were opposed by many of his contemporaries like Eddington and <a href="/wiki/Lev_Landau" title="Lev Landau">Lev Landau</a>, who argued that some yet unknown mechanism would stop the collapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> They were partly correct: a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> slightly more massive than the Chandrasekhar limit will collapse into a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> which is itself stable. But in 1939, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Oppenheimer" title="Robert Oppenheimer">Robert Oppenheimer</a> and others predicted that neutron stars above another limit (the <a href="/wiki/Tolman%E2%80%93Oppenheimer%E2%80%93Volkoff_limit" title="Tolman&#8211;Oppenheimer&#8211;Volkoff limit">Tolman&#8211;Oppenheimer&#8211;Volkoff limit</a>) would collapse further for the reasons presented by Chandrasekhar, and concluded that no law of physics was likely to intervene and stop at least some stars from collapsing to black holes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OV1939_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-OV1939-25">[25]</a></sup> Their original calculations, based on the <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a>, gave it as 0.7&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>; subsequent consideration of strong force-mediated neutron-neutron repulsion raised the estimate to approximately 1.5&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub> to 3.0&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bombaci_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bombaci-26">[26]</a></sup> Observations of the neutron star merger <a href="/wiki/GW170817" title="GW170817">GW170817</a>, which is thought to have generated a black hole shortly afterward, have refined the TOV limit estimate to ~2.17&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cho2018_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cho2018-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Margalit2017_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Margalit2017-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shibata2017_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shibata2017-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruiz2018_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruiz2018-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rezzolla2018_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rezzolla2018-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Oppenheimer and his co-authors interpreted the singularity at the boundary of the Schwarzschild radius as indicating that this was the boundary of a bubble in which time stopped. This is a valid point of view for external observers, but not for infalling observers. Because of this property, the collapsed stars were called "frozen stars", because an outside observer would see the surface of the star frozen in time at the instant where its collapse takes it to the Schwarzschild radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_age">Golden age</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_general_relativity" title="History of general relativity">History of general relativity</a></div>\n<p>In 1958, <a href="/wiki/David_Finkelstein" title="David Finkelstein">David Finkelstein</a> identified the Schwarzschild surface as an <a href="/wiki/Event_horizon" title="Event horizon">event horizon</a>, "a perfect unidirectional membrane: causal influences can cross it in only one direction".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> This did not strictly contradict Oppenheimer\'s results, but extended them to include the point of view of infalling observers. <a href="/wiki/Eddington%E2%80%93Finkelstein_coordinates" title="Eddington&#8211;Finkelstein coordinates">Finkelstein\'s solution</a> extended the Schwarzschild solution for the future of observers falling into a black hole. A <a href="/wiki/Kruskal%E2%80%93Szekeres_coordinates" title="Kruskal&#8211;Szekeres coordinates">complete extension</a> had already been found by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Kruskal" title="Martin Kruskal">Martin Kruskal</a>, who was urged to publish it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>These results came at the beginning of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_age_of_general_relativity" title="Golden age of general relativity">golden age of general relativity</a>, which was marked by general relativity and black holes becoming mainstream subjects of research. This process was helped by the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsars</a> in 1967,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> which, by 1969, were shown to be rapidly rotating <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-araa8_265_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-araa8_265-37">[37]</a></sup> Until that time, neutron stars, like black holes, were regarded as just theoretical curiosities; but the discovery of pulsars showed their physical relevance and spurred a further interest in all types of compact objects that might be formed by gravitational collapse.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>In this period more general black hole solutions were found. In 1963, <a href="/wiki/Roy_Kerr" title="Roy Kerr">Roy Kerr</a> found <a href="/wiki/Kerr_metric" title="Kerr metric">the exact solution</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Rotating_black_hole" title="Rotating black hole">rotating black hole</a>. Two years later, <a href="/wiki/Ezra_T._Newman" title="Ezra T. Newman">Ezra Newman</a> found the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axisymmetric" title="Axisymmetric">axisymmetric</a> solution for a black hole that is both rotating and <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electrically charged</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Through the work of <a href="/wiki/Werner_Israel" title="Werner Israel">Werner Israel</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brandon_Carter" title="Brandon Carter">Brandon Carter</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and David Robinson<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/No-hair_theorem" title="No-hair theorem">no-hair theorem</a> emerged, stating that a stationary black hole solution is completely described by the three parameters of the <a href="/wiki/Kerr%E2%80%93Newman_metric" title="Kerr&#8211;Newman metric">Kerr&#8211;Newman metric</a>: <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeuslerNoHair_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-HeuslerNoHair-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At first, it was suspected that the strange features of the black hole solutions were pathological artifacts from the symmetry conditions imposed, and that the singularities would not appear in generic situations. This view was held in particular by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vladimir_A._Belinsky" title="Vladimir A. Belinsky">Vladimir Belinsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaak_Markovich_Khalatnikov" title="Isaak Markovich Khalatnikov">Isaak Khalatnikov</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Evgeny_Lifshitz" title="Evgeny Lifshitz">Evgeny Lifshitz</a>, who tried to prove that no singularities appear in generic solutions. However, in the late 1960s <a href="/wiki/Roger_Penrose" title="Roger Penrose">Roger Penrose</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-penrose1965_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-penrose1965-44">[44]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a> used global techniques to prove that singularities appear generically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Work by <a href="/wiki/James_M._Bardeen" title="James M. Bardeen">James Bardeen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Bekenstein" title="Jacob Bekenstein">Jacob Bekenstein</a>, Carter, and Hawking in the early 1970s led to the formulation of <a href="/wiki/Black_hole_thermodynamics" title="Black hole thermodynamics">black hole thermodynamics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> These laws describe the behaviour of a black hole in close analogy to the <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_thermodynamics" title="Laws of thermodynamics">laws of thermodynamics</a> by relating mass to energy, area to <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Surface_gravity" title="Surface gravity">surface gravity</a> to <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>. The analogy was completed when Hawking, in 1974, showed that <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a> predicts that black holes should radiate like a <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black body</a> with a temperature proportional to the surface gravity of the black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawking1974_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawking1974-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>\n<p>John Michell used the term "dark star",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> and in the early 20th century, physicists used the term "gravitationally collapsed object".  Science writer Marcia Bartusiak traces the term "black hole" to physicist <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Dicke" title="Robert H. Dicke">Robert H. Dicke</a>, who in the early 1960s reportedly compared the phenomenon to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Hole_of_Calcutta" title="Black Hole of Calcutta">Black Hole of Calcutta</a>, notorious as a prison where people entered but never left alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartusiak_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartusiak-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The term "black hole" was used in print by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_magazine" title="Life magazine">Life magazine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_News" title="Science News">Science News</a> magazine in 1963,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartusiak_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bartusiak-49">[49]</a></sup> and by science journalist Ann Ewing in her article <span style="padding-right:.15em;">"</span>\'Black Holes\' in Space", dated 18 January 1964, which was a report on a meeting of the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="American Association for the Advancement of Science">American Association for the Advancement of Science</a> held in Cleveland, Ohio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scinewsewing_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-scinewsewing-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 1967, a student reportedly suggested the phrase "black hole" at a lecture by <a href="/wiki/John_Archibald_Wheeler" title="John Archibald Wheeler">John Wheeler</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scinewsewing_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-scinewsewing-50">[50]</a></sup> Wheeler adopted the term for its brevity and "advertising value", and it quickly caught on,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> leading some to credit Wheeler with coining the phrase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties_and_structure">Properties and structure</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Black_hole_details.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="672" data-file-width="746" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Black_hole_details.svg/220px-Black_hole_details.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Black_hole_details.svg/330px-Black_hole_details.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Black_hole_details.svg/440px-Black_hole_details.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Black_hole_details.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A simple illustration of a non-spinning black hole</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/No-hair_theorem" title="No-hair theorem">no-hair conjecture</a> postulates that, once it achieves a stable condition after formation, a black hole has only three independent physical properties: <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">charge</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>; the black hole is otherwise featureless. If the conjecture is true, any two black holes that share the same values for these properties, or parameters, are indistinguishable from one another. The degree to which the conjecture is true for real black holes under the laws of modern physics, is currently an unsolved problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeuslerNoHair_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-HeuslerNoHair-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>These properties are special because they are visible from outside a black hole. For example, a charged black hole repels other like charges just like any other charged object. Similarly, the total mass inside a sphere containing a black hole can be found by using the gravitational analog of <a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_law" title="Gauss\'s law">Gauss\'s law</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ADM_mass" title="ADM mass">ADM mass</a>, far away from the black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Likewise, the angular momentum can be measured from far away using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frame_dragging" title="Frame dragging">frame dragging</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitomagnetism" title="Gravitomagnetism">gravitomagnetic field</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>When an object falls into a black hole, any <a href="/wiki/Physical_information" title="Physical information">information</a> about the shape of the object or distribution of charge on it is evenly distributed along the horizon of the black hole, and is lost to outside observers. The behavior of the horizon in this situation is a <a href="/wiki/Dissipative_system" title="Dissipative system">dissipative system</a> that is closely analogous to that of a conductive stretchy membrane with friction and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">electrical resistance</a>&#8212;the <a href="/wiki/Membrane_paradigm" title="Membrane paradigm">membrane paradigm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> This is different from other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Field_theory_(physics)" title="Field theory (physics)">field theories</a> such as electromagnetism, which do not have any friction or resistivity at the microscopic level, because they are <a href="/wiki/T-symmetry" title="T-symmetry">time-reversible</a>. Because a black hole eventually achieves a stable state with only three parameters, there is no way to avoid losing information about the initial conditions: the gravitational and electric fields of a black hole give very little information about what went in. The information that is lost includes every quantity that cannot be measured far away from the black hole horizon, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_law_(physics)" title="Conservation law (physics)">approximately conserved</a> <a href="/wiki/Quantum_number" title="Quantum number">quantum numbers</a> such as the total <a href="/wiki/Baryon_number" title="Baryon number">baryon number</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lepton_number" title="Lepton number">lepton number</a>. This behavior is so puzzling that it has been called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_hole_information_loss_paradox" title="Black hole information loss paradox">black hole information loss paradox</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gravitational_time_dilation_around_a_black_hole.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="266" data-file-width="666" decoding="async" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Gravitational_time_dilation_around_a_black_hole.gif/220px-Gravitational_time_dilation_around_a_black_hole.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Gravitational_time_dilation_around_a_black_hole.gif/330px-Gravitational_time_dilation_around_a_black_hole.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Gravitational_time_dilation_around_a_black_hole.gif/440px-Gravitational_time_dilation_around_a_black_hole.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gravitational_time_dilation_around_a_black_hole.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gravitational time dilation around a black hole</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span></h3>\n<p>The simplest static black holes have mass but neither electric charge nor angular momentum. These black holes are often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Schwarzschild_metric" title="Schwarzschild metric">Schwarzschild black holes</a> after Karl Schwarzschild who discovered this <a href="/wiki/Solutions_of_the_Einstein_field_equations" title="Solutions of the Einstein field equations">solution</a> in 1916.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwarzschild1916_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schwarzschild1916-16">[16]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Birkhoff%27s_theorem_(relativity)" title="Birkhoff\'s theorem (relativity)">Birkhoff\'s theorem</a>, it is the only <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_solution_(general_relativity)" title="Vacuum solution (general relativity)">vacuum solution</a> that is <a href="/wiki/Spherically_symmetric_spacetime" title="Spherically symmetric spacetime">spherically symmetric</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> This means that there is no observable difference between the gravitational field of such a black hole and that of any other spherical object of the same mass. The popular notion of a black hole "sucking in everything" in its surroundings is therefore only correct near a black hole\'s horizon; far away, the external gravitational field is identical to that of any other body of the same mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Solutions describing more general black holes also exist. Non-rotating <a href="/wiki/Charged_black_hole" title="Charged black hole">charged black holes</a> are described by the <a href="/wiki/Reissner%E2%80%93Nordstr%C3%B6m_metric" title="Reissner&#8211;Nordstr&#246;m metric">Reissner&#8211;Nordstr&#246;m metric</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Kerr_metric" title="Kerr metric">Kerr metric</a> describes a non-charged <a href="/wiki/Rotating_black_hole" title="Rotating black hole">rotating black hole</a>. The most general <a href="/wiki/Stationary_spacetime" title="Stationary spacetime">stationary</a> black hole solution known is the <a href="/wiki/Kerr%E2%80%93Newman_metric" title="Kerr&#8211;Newman metric">Kerr&#8211;Newman metric</a>, which describes a black hole with both charge and angular momentum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro_teukolsky1983_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro_teukolsky1983-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the mass of a black hole can take any positive value, the charge and angular momentum are constrained by the mass. In <a href="/wiki/Planck_units" title="Planck units">Planck units</a>, the total electric charge&#160;<i>Q</i> and the total angular momentum&#160;<i>J</i> are expected to satisfy\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle Q^{2}+\\left({\\tfrac {J}{M}}\\right)^{2}\\leq M^{2}\\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mi>Q</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>J</mi>\n<mi>M</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>&#8804;<!-- &#8804; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle Q^{2}+\\left({\\tfrac {J}{M}}\\right)^{2}\\leq M^{2}\\,}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="Q^{2}+\\left({\\tfrac {J}{M}}\\right)^{2}\\leq M^{2}\\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68924b1e698031683e929676262873b353593c81" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:19.164ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>for a black hole of mass <i>M</i>. Black holes with the minimum possible mass satisfying this inequality are called <a href="/wiki/Extremal_black_hole" title="Extremal black hole">extremal</a>. Solutions of Einstein\'s equations that violate this inequality exist, but they do not possess an event horizon. These solutions have so-called <a href="/wiki/Naked_singularity" title="Naked singularity">naked singularities</a> that can be observed from the outside, and hence are deemed <i>unphysical</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_censorship_hypothesis" title="Cosmic censorship hypothesis">cosmic censorship hypothesis</a> rules out the formation of such singularities, when they are created through the gravitational collapse of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_conditions" title="Energy conditions">realistic matter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wald_1997_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-wald_1997-2">[2]</a></sup> This is supported by numerical simulations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Due to the relatively large strength of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetic force</a>, black holes forming from the collapse of stars are expected to retain the nearly neutral charge of the star. Rotation, however, is expected to be a universal feature of compact astrophysical objects. The black-hole candidate binary X-ray source <a href="/wiki/GRS_1915%2B105" title="GRS 1915+105">GRS 1915+105</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> appears to have an angular momentum near the maximum allowed value. That uncharged limit is<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50SMBH_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-50SMBH-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle J\\leq {\\frac {GM^{2}}{c}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>J</mi>\n<mo>&#8804;<!-- &#8804; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle J\\leq {\\frac {GM^{2}}{c}},}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle J\\leq {\\frac {GM^{2}}{c}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f39b611c84e512372f9abae625338cf27d8a4819" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:11.433ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>allowing definition of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dimensionless" title="Dimensionless">dimensionless</a> spin parameter such that<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50SMBH_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-50SMBH-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle 0\\leq {\\frac {cJ}{GM^{2}}}\\leq 1.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo>&#8804;<!-- &#8804; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mi>J</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8804;<!-- &#8804; --></mo>\n<mn>1.</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle 0\\leq {\\frac {cJ}{GM^{2}}}\\leq 1.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle 0\\leq {\\frac {cJ}{GM^{2}}}\\leq 1.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9316201c21641ceb0f7c92b2bdce82ba680da1f0" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:15.385ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50SMBH_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-50SMBH-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[Note 1]</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin:0 0 0.5em 1em;">\n<caption>Black hole classifications\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Class</th>\n<th>Approx.<br/>mass</th>\n<th>Approx.<br/>size\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">Supermassive black hole</a></td>\n<td style="text-align: center;">10<sup>5</sup>&#8211;10<sup>10</sup> <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><i>M</i><sub>Sun</sub></a></td>\n<td style="text-align: center;">0.001&#8211;400 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Intermediate-mass_black_hole" title="Intermediate-mass black hole">Intermediate-mass black hole</a></td>\n<td style="text-align: center;">10<sup>3</sup> <i>M</i><sub>Sun</sub></td>\n<td style="text-align: center;">10<sup>3</sup> km &#8776; <a href="/wiki/Earth_radius" title="Earth radius"><i>R</i><sub>Earth</sub></a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Stellar_black_hole" title="Stellar black hole">Stellar black hole</a></td>\n<td style="text-align: center;">10 <i>M</i><sub>Sun</sub></td>\n<td style="text-align: center;">30&#160;km\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Micro_black_hole" title="Micro black hole">Micro black hole</a></td>\n<td style="text-align: center;">up to <i>M</i><sub><a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a></sub></td>\n<td style="text-align: center;">up to 0.1&#160;mm\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Black holes are commonly classified according to their mass, independent of angular momentum, <i>J</i>. The size of a black hole, as determined by the radius of the event horizon, or <a href="/wiki/Schwarzschild_radius" title="Schwarzschild radius">Schwarzschild radius</a>, is roughly proportional to the mass, <i>M</i>, through\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle r_{\\mathrm {s} }={\\frac {2GM}{c^{2}}}\\approx 2.95\\,{\\frac {M}{M_{\\mathrm {Sun} }}}~\\mathrm {km,} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mi>M</mi>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<mn>2.95</mn>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<msub>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">u</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mtext>&#160;</mtext>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle r_{\\mathrm {s} }={\\frac {2GM}{c^{2}}}\\approx 2.95\\,{\\frac {M}{M_{\\mathrm {Sun} }}}~\\mathrm {km,} }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle r_{\\mathrm {s} }={\\frac {2GM}{c^{2}}}\\approx 2.95\\,{\\frac {M}{M_{\\mathrm {Sun} }}}~\\mathrm {km,} }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/63e419c8f80c25540acfc5246fa23f8d7da05e4e" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:29.37ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <i>r</i><sub>s</sub> is the Schwarzschild radius and <i>M</i><sub>Sun</sub> is the <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">mass of the Sun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> For a black hole with nonzero spin and/or electric charge, the radius is smaller,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[Note 2]</a></sup> until an <a href="/wiki/Extremal_black_hole" title="Extremal black hole">extremal black hole</a> could have an event horizon close to<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle r_{\\mathrm {+} }={\\frac {GM}{c^{2}}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>+</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mi>M</mi>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle r_{\\mathrm {+} }={\\frac {GM}{c^{2}}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle r_{\\mathrm {+} }={\\frac {GM}{c^{2}}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e593174ab3ec4c151765fbe61344fa85f9dbd173" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:11.41ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Event_horizon">Event horizon</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Event_horizon" title="Event horizon">Event horizon</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BH-no-escape-1.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="106" data-file-width="409" decoding="async" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/BH-no-escape-1.svg/300px-BH-no-escape-1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/BH-no-escape-1.svg/450px-BH-no-escape-1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/BH-no-escape-1.svg/600px-BH-no-escape-1.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Far away from the black hole, a particle can move in any direction, as illustrated by the set of arrows. It is only restricted by the speed of light.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BH-no-escape-2.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="106" data-file-width="409" decoding="async" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/BH-no-escape-2.svg/300px-BH-no-escape-2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/BH-no-escape-2.svg/450px-BH-no-escape-2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/BH-no-escape-2.svg/600px-BH-no-escape-2.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Closer to the black hole, spacetime starts to deform. There are more paths going towards the black hole than paths moving away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[Note 3]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BH-no-escape-3.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="106" data-file-width="409" decoding="async" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/BH-no-escape-3.svg/300px-BH-no-escape-3.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/BH-no-escape-3.svg/450px-BH-no-escape-3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/BH-no-escape-3.svg/600px-BH-no-escape-3.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Inside of the event horizon, all paths bring the particle closer to the center of the black hole. It is no longer possible for the particle to escape.</div></div></div></div>\n<p>The defining feature of a black hole is the appearance of an event horizon&#8212;a boundary in <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a> through which matter and light can only pass inward towards the mass of the black hole. Nothing, not even light, can escape from inside the event horizon. The event horizon is referred to as such because if an event occurs within the boundary, information from that event cannot reach an outside observer, making it impossible to determine if such an event occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As predicted by general relativity, the presence of a mass deforms spacetime in such a way that the paths taken by particles bend towards the mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup> At the event horizon of a black hole, this deformation becomes so strong that there are no paths that lead away from the black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To a distant observer, clocks near a black hole would appear to tick more slowly than those further away from the black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> Due to this effect, known as <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_time_dilation" title="Gravitational time dilation">gravitational time dilation</a>, an object falling into a black hole appears to slow as it approaches the event horizon, taking an infinite time to reach it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> At the same time, all processes on this object slow down, from the view point of a fixed outside observer, causing any light emitted by the object to appear redder and dimmer, an effect known as <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_redshift" title="Gravitational redshift">gravitational redshift</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> Eventually, the falling object fades away until it can no longer be seen. Typically this process happens very rapidly with an object disappearing from view within less than a second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the other hand, indestructible observers falling into a black hole do not notice any of these effects as they cross the event horizon. According to their own clocks, which appear to them to tick normally, they cross the event horizon after a finite time without noting any singular behaviour; in classical general relativity, it is impossible to determine the location of the event horizon from local observations, due to Einstein\'s <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">equivalence principle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The shape of the event horizon of a black hole is always approximately spherical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[Note 4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[79]</a></sup> For non-rotating (static) black holes the geometry of the event horizon is precisely spherical, while for rotating black holes the event horizon is oblate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[80]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singularity">Singularity</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_singularity" title="Gravitational singularity">Gravitational singularity</a></div>\n<p>At the center of a black hole, as described by general relativity, lies a <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_singularity" title="Gravitational singularity">gravitational singularity</a>, a region where the spacetime curvature becomes infinite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[81]</a></sup> For a non-rotating black hole, this region takes the shape of a single point and for a <a href="/wiki/Rotating_black_hole" title="Rotating black hole">rotating black hole</a>, it is smeared out to form a <a href="/wiki/Ring_singularity" title="Ring singularity">ring singularity</a> that lies in the plane of rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup> In both cases, the singular region has zero volume. It can also be shown that the singular region contains all the mass of the black hole solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[83]</a></sup> The singular region can thus be thought of as having infinite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_density" title="Mass density">density</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Observers falling into a Schwarzschild black hole (<i>i.e.</i>, non-rotating and not charged) cannot avoid being carried into the singularity, once they cross the event horizon. They can prolong the experience by accelerating away to slow their descent, but only up to a limit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[85]</a></sup> When they reach the singularity, they are crushed to infinite density and their mass is added to the total of the black hole. Before that happens, they will have been torn apart by the growing <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal forces</a> in a process sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Spaghettification" title="Spaghettification">spaghettification</a> or the "noodle effect".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the case of a charged (Reissner&#8211;Nordstr&#246;m) or rotating (Kerr) black hole, it is possible to avoid the singularity. Extending these solutions as far as possible reveals the hypothetical possibility of exiting the black hole into a different spacetime with the black hole acting as a <a href="/wiki/Wormhole" title="Wormhole">wormhole</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[87]</a></sup> The possibility of traveling to another universe is, however, only theoretical since any perturbation would destroy this possibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[88]</a></sup> It also appears to be possible to follow <a href="/wiki/Closed_timelike_curve" title="Closed timelike curve">closed timelike curves</a> (returning to one\'s own past) around the Kerr singularity, which lead to problems with <a href="/wiki/Causality_(physics)" title="Causality (physics)">causality</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Grandfather_paradox" title="Grandfather paradox">grandfather paradox</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[89]</a></sup> It is expected that none of these peculiar effects would survive in a proper quantum treatment of rotating and charged black holes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The appearance of singularities in general relativity is commonly perceived as signaling the breakdown of the theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[91]</a></sup> This breakdown, however, is expected; it occurs in a situation where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_effects" title="Quantum effects">quantum effects</a> should describe these actions, due to the extremely high density and therefore particle interactions. To date, it has not been possible to combine quantum and gravitational effects into a single theory, although there exist attempts to formulate such a theory of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravity</a>. It is generally expected that such a theory will not feature any singularities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photon_sphere">Photon sphere</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Photon_sphere" title="Photon sphere">Photon sphere</a></div>\n<p>The photon sphere is a spherical boundary of zero thickness in which <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> that move on <a href="/wiki/Tangent" title="Tangent">tangents</a> to that sphere would be trapped in a circular orbit about the black hole. For non-rotating black holes, the photon sphere has a radius 1.5 times the Schwarzschild radius. Their orbits would be <a href="/wiki/Instability" title="Instability">dynamically unstable</a>, hence any small perturbation, such as a particle of infalling matter, would cause an instability that would grow over time, either setting the photon on an outward trajectory causing it to escape the black hole, or on an inward spiral where it would eventually cross the event horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prd84_6_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-prd84_6-98">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While light can still escape from the photon sphere, any light that crosses the photon sphere on an inbound trajectory will be captured by the black hole. Hence any light that reaches an outside observer from the photon sphere must have been emitted by objects between the photon sphere and the event horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prd84_6_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-prd84_6-98">[94]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ergosphere">Ergosphere</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ergosphere" title="Ergosphere">Ergosphere</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ergosphere_and_event_horizon_of_a_rotating_black_hole_(no_animation).gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="494" data-file-width="494" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Ergosphere_and_event_horizon_of_a_rotating_black_hole_%28no_animation%29.gif/220px-Ergosphere_and_event_horizon_of_a_rotating_black_hole_%28no_animation%29.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Ergosphere_and_event_horizon_of_a_rotating_black_hole_%28no_animation%29.gif/330px-Ergosphere_and_event_horizon_of_a_rotating_black_hole_%28no_animation%29.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Ergosphere_and_event_horizon_of_a_rotating_black_hole_%28no_animation%29.gif/440px-Ergosphere_and_event_horizon_of_a_rotating_black_hole_%28no_animation%29.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ergosphere_and_event_horizon_of_a_rotating_black_hole_(no_animation).gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ergosphere is a pumpkin-shaped region outside of the event horizon, where objects cannot remain stationary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-viss_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-viss-99">[95]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Rotating black holes are surrounded by a region of spacetime in which it is impossible to stand still, called the ergosphere. This is the result of a process known as <a href="/wiki/Frame-dragging" title="Frame-dragging">frame-dragging</a>; general relativity predicts that any rotating mass will tend to slightly "drag" along the spacetime immediately surrounding it. Any object near the rotating mass will tend to start moving in the direction of rotation. For a rotating black hole, this effect is so strong near the event horizon that an object would have to move faster than the speed of light in the opposite direction to just stand still.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ergosphere of a black hole is a volume whose inner boundary is the black hole\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">oblate spheroid</a> event horizon and a pumpkin-shaped outer boundary, which coincides with the event horizon at the poles but noticeably wider around the equator. The outer boundary is sometimes called the <i>ergosurface</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-viss_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-viss-99">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Objects and radiation can escape normally from the ergosphere. Through the <a href="/wiki/Penrose_process" title="Penrose process">Penrose process</a>, objects can emerge from the ergosphere with more energy than they entered. This energy is taken from the rotational energy of the black hole causing the latter to slow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Innermost_stable_circular_orbit_.28ISCO.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Innermost_stable_circular_orbit_(ISCO)">Innermost stable circular orbit (ISCO)</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Innermost_stable_circular_orbit" title="Innermost stable circular orbit">Innermost stable circular orbit</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton\'s law of universal gravitation">Newtonian gravity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Test_particle" title="Test particle">test particles</a> can stably orbit at arbitrary distances from a central object. In <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, however, there exists an innermost stable circular orbit (often called the ISCO), inside of which, any infinitesimal perturbations to a circular orbit will lead to inspiral into the black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup> The location of the ISCO depends on the spin of the black hole, in the case of a Schwarzschild black hole (spin zero) is:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle r_{isco}=3\\,r_{s}={\\frac {6\\,GM}{c^{2}}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>s</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mi>o</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>3</mn>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<msub>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>s</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mn>6</mn>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mi>M</mi>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle r_{isco}=3\\,r_{s}={\\frac {6\\,GM}{c^{2}}},}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle r_{isco}=3\\,r_{s}={\\frac {6\\,GM}{c^{2}}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/24233510338b3dbc1f5149e3c822fc672adf8e89" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:21.229ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>and decreases with increasing black hole spin for particles orbiting in the same direction as the spin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_evolution">Formation and evolution</span></h2>\n<p>Given the bizarre character of black holes, it was long questioned whether such objects could actually exist in nature or whether they were merely pathological solutions to Einstein\'s equations.  Einstein himself wrongly thought that black holes would not form, because he held that the angular momentum of collapsing particles would stabilize their motion at some radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup> This led the general relativity community to dismiss all results to the contrary for many years. However, a minority of relativists continued to contend that black holes were physical objects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[101]</a></sup> and by the end of the 1960s, they had persuaded the majority of researchers in the field that there is no obstacle to the formation of an event horizon.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/BBH_gravitational_lensing_of_gw150914.webm/854px--BBH_gravitational_lensing_of_gw150914.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="34.999" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="BBH_gravitational_lensing_of_gw150914.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/a4/BBH_gravitational_lensing_of_gw150914.webm/BBH_gravitational_lensing_of_gw150914.webm.480p.webm" type="video/webm;
